<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chuhuo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Chuhuo.jpg/220px-Chuhuo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Chuhuo.jpg/330px-Chuhuo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Chuhuo.jpg/440px-Chuhuo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chuhuo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Natural gas coming out of the ground in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Natural gas was discovered accidentally in ancient China, as it resulted from the drilling for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brine_(solution)" title="Brine (solution)">brines</a>.  Natural gas was first used by the Chinese in about 500 BC (possibly even 1000 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>). They discovered a way to transport gas seeping from the ground in crude pipelines of bamboo to where it was used to boil salt water to <a href="/wiki/Salt_in_Chinese_history" title="Salt in Chinese history">extract the salt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Ziliujing_District" title="Ziliujing District">Ziliujing District</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The world's first industrial extraction of natural gas started at <a href="/wiki/Fredonia,_New_York" title="Fredonia, New York">Fredonia, New York</a>, United States, in 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> By 2009, 66 000 km³ (or 8%) had been used out of the total 850 000 km³ of estimated remaining recoverable reserves of natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldenergyoutlook.org_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldenergyoutlook.org-12">[12]</a></sup> Based on an estimated 2015 world consumption rate of about 3400 km³ of gas per year, the total estimated remaining economically recoverable reserves of natural gas would last 250 years at current consumption rates. An annual increase in usage of 2–3% could result in currently recoverable reserves lasting significantly less, perhaps as few as 80 to 100 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldenergyoutlook.org_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-worldenergyoutlook.org-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_gas_fields" title="List of natural gas fields">List of natural gas fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_proven_reserves" title="List of countries by natural gas proven reserves">List of countries by natural gas proven reserves</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_production" title="List of countries by natural gas production">List of countries by natural gas production</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_gas">Natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="1872" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg/170px-BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg/255px-BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg/340px-BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Natural gas <a href="/wiki/Drilling_rig" title="Drilling rig">drilling rig</a> in Texas.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 19th century, natural gas was usually obtained as a by-product of <a href="/wiki/Oil_well" title="Oil well">producing oil</a>, since the small, light gas carbon chains came out of solution as the extracted fluids underwent pressure reduction from the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_reservoir" title="Petroleum reservoir">reservoir</a> to the surface, similar to uncapping a soft drink bottle where the carbon dioxide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effervesce" title="Effervesce">effervesces</a>. Unwanted natural gas was a disposal problem in the active oil fields. If there was not a market for natural gas near the <a href="/wiki/Wellhead" title="Wellhead">wellhead</a> it was prohibitively expensive to pipe to the end user.
</p><p>In the 19th century and early 20th century, unwanted gas was usually <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">burned off at oil fields</a>. Today, unwanted gas (or <a href="/wiki/Stranded_gas_reserve" title="Stranded gas reserve">stranded gas</a> without a market) associated with oil extraction often is returned to the reservoir with 'injection' wells while awaiting a possible future market or to repressurize the formation, which can enhance extraction rates from other wells. In regions with a high natural gas demand (such as the US), <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a> are constructed when it is economically feasible to transport gas from a wellsite to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End_consumer" title="End consumer">end consumer</a>.
</p><p>In addition to transporting gas via pipelines for use in power generation, other end uses for natural gas include export as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LNG" title="LNG">liquefied natural gas</a> (LNG) or conversion of natural gas into other liquid products via <a href="/wiki/Gas_to_liquids" title="Gas to liquids">gas to liquids</a> (GTL) technologies. GTL technologies can convert natural gas into liquids products such as gasoline, diesel or jet fuel. A variety of GTL technologies have been developed, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fischer%E2%80%93Tropsch" title="Fischer–Tropsch">Fischer–Tropsch</a> (F–T), methanol to gasoline (MTG) and <a href="/wiki/Syngas_to_gasoline_plus" title="Syngas to gasoline plus">syngas to gasoline plus</a> (STG+). F–T produces a synthetic crude that can be further refined into finished products, while MTG can produce synthetic gasoline from natural gas. STG+ can produce drop-in gasoline, diesel, jet fuel and aromatic chemicals directly from natural gas via a single-loop process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STG+_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-STG+-13">[13]</a></sup> In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Dutch_Shell" title="Royal Dutch Shell">Royal Dutch Shell's</a> 140,000 barrels (22,000 m<sup>3</sup>) per day F–T plant went into operation in <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas can be "associated" (found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a>), or "non-associated" (isolated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_field" title="Natural gas field">natural gas fields</a>), and is also found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_bed" title="Coal bed">coal beds</a> (as <a href="/wiki/Coalbed_methane" title="Coalbed methane">coalbed methane</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> It sometimes contains a significant amount of <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a>—heavier hydrocarbons removed for commercial use prior to the <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> being sold as a consumer fuel or chemical plant feedstock. Non-hydrocarbons such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> (rarely), and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> must also be removed before the natural gas can be transported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas extracted from oil wells is called casinghead gas (whether or not truly produced up the annulus and through a casinghead outlet) or associated gas. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_industry" title="Natural gas industry">natural gas industry</a> is extracting an increasing quantity of gas from challenging <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_petroleum_industry_in_Canada_(natural_gas)#Unconventional_gas" title="History of the petroleum industry in Canada (natural gas)">resource types</a>: <a href="/wiki/Sour_gas" title="Sour gas">sour gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tight_gas" title="Tight gas">tight gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">shale gas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coalbed_methane" title="Coalbed methane">coalbed methane</a>.
</p><p>There is some disagreement on which country has the largest proven gas reserves. Sources that consider that Russia has by far the largest proven reserves include the US CIA (47 600 km³),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> the US Energy Information Administration (47 800 km³),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (November 2017)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and OPEC (48 700 km³).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> However, BP credits Russia with only 32 900 km³,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> which would place it in second place, slightly behind Iran (33 100 to 33 800 km³, depending on the source).  With <a href="/wiki/Gazprom" title="Gazprom">Gazprom</a>, Russia is frequently the world's largest natural gas extractor. Major proven resources (in cubic kilometers) are world 187 300 (2013), Iran 33 600 (2013), Russia 32 900 (2013), Qatar 25 100 (2013), Turkmenistan 17 500 (2013) and the United States 8500 (2013).
</p><p>It is estimated that there are about 900 000 km³ of "unconventional" gas such as shale gas, of which 180 000 km³ may be recoverable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In turn, many studies from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIT" title="MIT">MIT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_%26_Veatch" title="Black &amp; Veatch">Black &amp; Veatch</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">DOE</a> predict that natural gas will account for a larger portion of electricity generation and heat in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The world's largest gas field is the offshore <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Pars_/_North_Dome_Gas-Condensate_field" title="South Pars / North Dome Gas-Condensate field">South Pars / North Dome Gas-Condensate field</a>, shared between Iran and Qatar. It is estimated to have 51,000 cubic kilometers (12,000 cu mi) of natural gas and 50 billion barrels (7.9 billion cubic meters) of <a href="/wiki/Natural-gas_condensate" title="Natural-gas condensate">natural gas condensates</a>.
</p><p>Because natural gas is not a pure product, as the reservoir pressure drops when non-associated gas is extracted from a field under <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a> (pressure/temperature) conditions, the higher molecular weight components may partially condense upon isothermic depressurizing—an effect called <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_condensation" title="Retrograde condensation">retrograde condensation</a>. The liquid thus formed may get trapped as the pores of the gas reservoir get depleted. One method to deal with this problem is to re-inject dried gas free of condensate to maintain the underground pressure and to allow re-evaporation and extraction of condensates. More frequently, the liquid condenses at the surface, and one of the tasks of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_processing" title="Natural gas processing">gas plant</a> is to collect this condensate. The resulting liquid is called natural gas liquid (NGL) and has commercial value.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shale_gas">Shale gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Shale gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Globe icon." data-file-height="290" data-file-width="350" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/48px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/73px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/97px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 2x" width="48"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this article <b>deal primarily with the United States and do not represent a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the subject</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You may <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a>, discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Natural_gas" title="Talk:Natural gas">talk page</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_wizard" title="Wikipedia:Article wizard">create a new article</a>, as appropriate.</span> <small><i>(September 2013)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GasDepositDiagram.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="473" data-file-width="718" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/GasDepositDiagram.jpg/440px-GasDepositDiagram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/GasDepositDiagram.jpg/660px-GasDepositDiagram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/GasDepositDiagram.jpg 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GasDepositDiagram.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The location of <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">shale gas</a> compared to other types of gas deposits.</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">Shale gas</a></div>
<p>Shale gas is natural gas produced from <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a>. Because shale has matrix permeability too low to allow gas to flow in economical quantities, shale gas wells depend on fractures to allow the gas to flow. Early shale gas wells depended on natural fractures through which gas flowed; almost all shale gas wells today require fractures artificially created by <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a>. Since 2000, shale gas has become a major source of natural gas in the United States and Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Because of increased shale gas production, the United States is now the number one natural gas producer in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Following the success in the United States, shale gas exploration is beginning in countries such as Poland, China, and South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Town_gas">Town gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Town gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_manufactured_gas" title="History of manufactured gas">History of manufactured gas</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Town_gas" title="Town gas">Town gas</a> is a flammable gaseous fuel made by the destructive distillation of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>. It contains a variety of calorific gases including <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, and other volatile <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a>, together with small quantities of non-calorific gases such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and is used in a similar way to natural gas. This is a historical technology and is not usually economically competitive with other sources of fuel gas today.
</p><p>Most town "gashouses" located in the eastern US in the late 19th and early 20th centuries were simple by-product <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> ovens that heated bituminous coal in air-tight chambers. The gas driven off from the coal was collected and distributed through networks of pipes to residences and other buildings where it was used for cooking and lighting. (Gas heating did not come into widespread use until the last half of the 20th century.) The <a href="/wiki/Coal_tar" title="Coal tar">coal tar</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a>) that collected in the bottoms of the gashouse ovens was often used for roofing and other waterproofing purposes, and when mixed with sand and gravel was used for paving streets.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biogas">Biogas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biogas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">Biogas</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">Methanogenic archaea</a> are responsible for all biological sources of methane. Some live in symbiotic relationships with other life forms, including <a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">termites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminants</a>, and cultivated crops. Other sources of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, the principal component of natural gas, include <a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a>, biogas, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methane_hydrate" title="Methane hydrate">methane hydrate</a>. When methane-rich gases are produced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_decay" title="Anaerobic decay">anaerobic decay</a> of non-fossil <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> matter (<a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>), these are referred to as biogas (or natural biogas). Sources of biogas include <a href="/wiki/Swamp" title="Swamp">swamps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">landfills</a>, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural_waste" title="Agricultural waste">agricultural waste</a> materials such as <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> <a href="/wiki/Sludge" title="Sludge">sludge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> by way of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digester" title="Anaerobic digester">anaerobic digesters</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> in addition to <a href="/wiki/Enteric_fermentation" title="Enteric fermentation">enteric fermentation</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>. Landfill gas is created by decomposition of waste in <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">landfill</a> sites. Excluding <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>, about half of landfill gas is methane and most of the rest is <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, with small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, and variable trace amounts of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siloxane" title="Siloxane">siloxanes</a>. If the gas is not removed, the pressure may get so high that it works its way to the surface, causing damage to the landfill structure, unpleasant odor, vegetation die-off, and an <a href="/wiki/Explosion" title="Explosion">explosion</a> hazard. The gas can be vented to the atmosphere, <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">flared</a> or burned to produce <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> or <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a>. Biogas can also be produced by separating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">organic materials</a> from waste that otherwise goes to landfills. This method is more efficient than just capturing the landfill gas it produces. <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_lagoon" title="Anaerobic lagoon">Anaerobic lagoons</a> produce biogas from manure, while biogas reactors can be used for manure or plant parts. Like landfill gas, biogas is mostly methane and carbon dioxide, with small amounts of nitrogen, oxygen and hydrogen. However, with the exception of pesticides, there are usually lower levels of contaminants.
</p><p>Landfill gas cannot be distributed through utility natural gas pipelines unless it is cleaned up to less than 3% <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, and a few parts per million <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span>, because <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span> corrode the pipelines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The presence of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> will lower the energy level of the gas below requirements for the pipeline.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  Siloxanes in the gas will form deposits in gas burners and need to be removed prior to entry into any gas distribution or transmission system. Consequently, it may be more economical to burn the gas on site or within a short distance of the landfill using a dedicated pipeline. Water vapor is often removed, even if the gas is burned on site.  If low temperatures condense water out of the gas, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siloxanes" title="Siloxanes">siloxanes</a> can be lowered as well because they tend to condense out with the water vapor. Other non-methane components may also be removed to meet <a href="/wiki/Emission_standard" title="Emission standard">emission standards</a>, to prevent fouling of the equipment or for environmental considerations. Co-firing landfill gas with natural gas improves combustion, which lowers emissions.
</p><p>Biogas, and especially landfill gas, are already used in some areas, but their use could be greatly expanded. Experimental systems were being proposed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (June 2013)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> for use in parts of <a href="/wiki/Hertfordshire" title="Hertfordshire">Hertfordshire</a>, UK, and <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a> in France.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Using materials that would otherwise generate no income, or even cost money to get rid of, improves the profitability and energy balance of biogas production. Gas generated in <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a> plants is commonly used to generate electricity. For example, the Hyperion sewage plant in Los Angeles burns 8 million cubic feet (230,000 cubic meters) of gas per day to generate power<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> New York City utilizes gas to run equipment in the sewage plants, to generate electricity, and in boilers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Using sewage gas to make electricity is not limited to large cities. The city of <a href="/wiki/Bakersfield,_California" title="Bakersfield, California">Bakersfield, California</a>, uses <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">cogeneration</a> at its sewer plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> California has 242 sewage wastewater treatment plants, 74 of which have installed anaerobic digesters. The total biopower generation from the 74 plants is about 66 MW.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Crystallized_natural_gas_.E2.80.94_hydrates"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Crystallized_natural_gas_—_hydrates">Crystallized natural gas — hydrates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Crystallized natural gas — hydrates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Huge quantities of natural gas (primarily methane) exist in the form of <a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">hydrates</a> under sediment on offshore continental shelves and on land in arctic regions that experience <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a>, such as those in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>. Hydrates require a combination of high pressure and low temperature to form.
</p><p>In 2010, the cost of extracting natural gas from crystallized natural gas was estimated to be as much as twice the cost of extracting natural gas from conventional sources, and even higher from offshore deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, Japan Oil, Gas and Metals National Corporation (JOGMEC) announced that they had recovered commercially relevant quantities of natural gas from methane hydrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NaturalGasProcessingPlant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="197" data-file-width="299" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/NaturalGasProcessingPlant.jpg/220px-NaturalGasProcessingPlant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/NaturalGasProcessingPlant.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NaturalGasProcessingPlant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The McMahon natural gas processing plant in <a href="/wiki/Taylor,_British_Columbia" title="Taylor, British Columbia">Taylor, British Columbia</a>, Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Processing_Natural_Gas_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Processing_Natural_Gas-36">[36]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_gas_processing">Natural gas processing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Natural gas processing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_processing" title="Natural gas processing">Natural gas processing</a></div>
<p>The image below is a schematic <a href="/wiki/Process_flow_diagram" title="Process flow diagram">block flow diagram</a> of a typical natural gas processing plant. It shows the various unit processes used to convert raw natural gas into sales gas pipelined to the end user markets.
</p><p>The block flow diagram also shows how processing of the raw natural gas yields byproduct sulfur, byproduct ethane, and natural gas liquids (NGL) propane, butanes and natural gasoline (denoted as <a href="/wiki/Pentanes" title="Pentanes">pentanes</a> +).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:746px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NatGasProcessing.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="524" data-file-width="744" height="524" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/NatGasProcessing.svg/744px-NatGasProcessing.svg.png" width="744"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption">Schematic flow diagram of a typical natural gas processing plant.</div></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Depletion">Depletion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Depletion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gas_depletion" title="Gas depletion">Gas depletion</a></div>
<p>Natural gas production in the US reached a peak in 1973, and went over a second lower peak in 2001, but recently has peaked again and is continuing to rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage_and_transport">Storage and transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Storage and transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1166" data-file-width="844" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg/170px-Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg/255px-Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg/340px-Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polyethylene_gas_main.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">Polyethylene</a> plastic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_main" title="Gas main">main</a> being placed in a <a href="/wiki/Trench" title="Trench">trench</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Because of its low density, it is not easy to store natural gas or to transport it by vehicle. Natural gas <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a> are impractical across oceans, since the gas needs to be cooled down and compressed, as the friction in the pipeline causes the gas to heat up. Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_gas_pipelines#North_America" title="List of natural gas pipelines">existing pipelines in America</a> are close to reaching their capacity, prompting some politicians representing northern states to speak of potential shortages. The large trade cost implies that natural gas markets are globally much less integrated, causing significant price differences across countries. In <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, the gas pipeline network is already dense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (November 2017)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (November 2017)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> New pipelines are planned or under construction in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> and between gas fields in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Africa" title="Northern Africa">Northern Africa</a> and Western Europe. See also <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_gas_pipelines" title="List of natural gas pipelines">List of natural gas pipelines</a>.
</p><p>Whenever gas is bought or sold at custody transfer points, rules and agreements are made regarding the gas quality. These may include the maximum allowable concentration of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span></a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor"><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></a>. Usually sales quality gas that has been <a href="/wiki/Natural-gas_processing" title="Natural-gas processing">treated to remove contamination</a> is traded on a "dry gas" basis and is required to be commercially free from objectionable odours, materials, and dust or other solid or liquid matter, waxes, gums and gum forming constituents, which might damage or adversely affect operation of equipment downstream of the custody transfer point.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/LNG_carrier" title="LNG carrier">LNG carriers</a> transport liquefied natural gas (LNG) across oceans, while <a href="/wiki/Tank_truck" title="Tank truck">tank trucks</a> can carry liquefied or compressed natural gas (CNG) over shorter distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Sea transport using <a href="/wiki/CNG_carrier" title="CNG carrier">CNG carrier</a> ships that are now under development may be competitive with LNG transport in specific conditions.
</p><p>Gas is turned into liquid at a <a href="/wiki/Liquefaction_of_gases" title="Liquefaction of gases">liquefaction</a> plant, and is returned to gas form at <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">regasification</a> plant at the <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas_terminal" title="Liquefied natural gas terminal">terminal</a>. Shipborne regasification equipment is also used. LNG is the preferred form for long distance, high volume transportation of natural gas, whereas pipeline is preferred for transport for distances up to 4,000 km (2,500 mi) over land and approximately half that distance offshore.
</p><p>CNG is transported at high pressure, typically above 200 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bars</a> (20,000 kPa; 2,900 psi). Compressors and decompression equipment are less capital intensive and may be economical in smaller unit sizes than liquefaction/regasification plants. Natural gas trucks and carriers may transport natural gas directly to end-users, or to distribution points such as pipelines.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1506" data-file-width="2363" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg/220px-Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg/330px-Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg/440px-Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manlove_gas_storage_facility_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peoples_Gas" title="Peoples Gas">Peoples Gas</a> Manlove Field <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_storage" title="Natural gas storage">natural gas storage</a> area in <a href="/wiki/Newcomb_Township,_Champaign_County,_Illinois" title="Newcomb Township, Champaign County, Illinois">Newcomb Township, Champaign County, Illinois</a>. In the foreground (left) is one of the numerous wells for the underground storage area, with an LNG plant, and above ground storage tanks are in the background (right).</div></div></div>
<p>In the past, the natural gas which was recovered in the course of recovering <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> could not be profitably sold, and was simply burned at the oil field in a process known as <a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">flaring</a>. Flaring is now illegal in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Additionally, higher demand in the last 20–30 years has made production of gas associated with oil economically viable. As a further option, the gas is now sometimes re-<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/inject" title="wiktionary:inject">injected</a> into the formation for <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> by pressure maintenance as well as miscible or immiscible flooding. Conservation, re-injection, or flaring of natural gas associated with oil is primarily dependent on proximity to markets (pipelines), and regulatory restrictions.
</p><p>Natural gas can be indirectly exported through the absorption in other physical output. A recent study suggests that the expansion of shale gas production in the US has caused prices to drop relative to other countries. This has caused  a boom in energy intensive manufacturing sector exports, whereby the average dollar unit of US manufacturing exports has almost tripled its energy content between 1996 and 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>A "master gas system" was invented in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> in the late 1970s, ending any necessity for flaring. Satellite observation, however, shows that flaring<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and venting<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> are still practiced in some gas-extracting countries.
</p><p>Natural gas is used to generate electricity and heat for <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalination</a>. Similarly, some landfills that also discharge methane gases have been set up to capture the methane and generate electricity.
</p><p>Natural gas is often stored underground inside depleted gas reservoirs from previous gas wells, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_domes" title="Salt domes">salt domes</a>, or in tanks as liquefied natural gas.  The gas is injected in a time of low demand and extracted when demand picks up. Storage nearby end users helps to meet volatile demands, but such storage may not always be practicable.
</p><p>With 15 countries accounting for 84% of the worldwide extraction, access to natural gas has become an important issue in international politics, and countries vie for control of pipelines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In the first decade of the 21st century, <a href="/wiki/Gazprom" title="Gazprom">Gazprom</a>, the state-owned energy company in Russia, engaged in disputes with <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> over the price of natural gas, which have created concerns that gas deliveries to parts of Europe could be cut off for political reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The United States is preparing to export natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Floating_liquefied_natural_gas">Floating liquefied natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Floating liquefied natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Floating_liquefied_natural_gas" title="Floating liquefied natural gas">Floating liquefied natural gas</a> (FLNG) is an innovative technology designed to enable the development of offshore gas resources that would otherwise remain untapped due to environmental or economic factors it is nonviable to develop them via a land-based LNG operation. FLNG technology also provides a number of environmental and economic advantages:
</p>
<ul><li>Environmental – Because all processing is done at the gas field, there is no requirement for long pipelines to shore, compression units to pump the gas to shore, dredging and jetty construction, and onshore construction of an LNG processing plant, which significantly reduces the environmental footprint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Avoiding construction also helps preserve marine and coastal environments. In addition, environmental disturbance will be minimised during decommissioning because the facility can easily be disconnected and removed before being refurbished and re-deployed elsewhere.</li>
<li>Economic – Where pumping gas to shore can be prohibitively expensive, FLNG makes development economically viable. As a result, it will open up new business opportunities for countries to develop offshore gas fields that would otherwise remain stranded, such as those offshore East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Many gas and oil companies are considering the economic and environmental benefits of floating liquefied natural gas (FLNG). There are currently projects underway to construct five FLNG facilities. <a href="/wiki/Petronas" title="Petronas">Petronas</a> is close to completion on their FLNG-1<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daewoo_Shipbuilding_and_Marine_Engineering" title="Daewoo Shipbuilding and Marine Engineering">Daewoo Shipbuilding and Marine Engineering</a> and are underway on their FLNG-2 project<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Heavy_Industries" title="Samsung Heavy Industries">Samsung Heavy Industries</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shell_Prelude" title="Shell Prelude">Shell Prelude</a> is due to start production 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Browse_LNG" title="Browse LNG">Browse LNG</a> project will commence <a href="/wiki/Front-end_loading" title="Front-end loading">FEED</a> in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Natural gas is primarily used in the northern hemisphere. North America and Europe are major consumers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-stream_natural_gas">Mid-stream natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mid-stream natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Often well head gases require removal of various hydrocarbon molecules contained within the gas. Some of these gases include <a href="/wiki/Heptane" title="Heptane">heptane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> and other hydrocarbons with molecular weights above <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>). The natural gas transmission lines extend to the natural gas processing plant or unit which removes the higher molecular weighted hydrocarbons to produce natural gas with energy content between 950–1,050 British thermal units per cubic foot (35–39 MJ/m<sup>3</sup>). The processed natural gas may then be used for residential, commercial and industrial uses.
</p><p>Natural gas flowing in the distribution lines is called mid-stream natural gas and is often used to power engines which rotate compressors. These compressors are required in the transmission line to pressurize and re-pressurize the mid-stream natural gas as the gas travels. Typically, natural gas powered engines require 950–1,050 BTU/cu ft (35–39 MJ/m<sup>3</sup>) natural gas to operate at the rotational name plate specifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-american-environmental_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-american-environmental-60">[60]</a></sup> Several methods are used to remove these higher molecular weighted gases for use by the natural gas engine. A few technologies are as follows:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Joule%E2%80%93Thomson_effect" title="Joule–Thomson effect">Joule–Thomson skid</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">Cryogenic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chiller" title="Chiller">chiller</a> system</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">Chemical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzymology" title="Enzymology">enzymology</a> system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-american-environmental_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-american-environmental-60">[60]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_generation">Power generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Power generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas is a major source of <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a> through the use of <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">cogeneration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbines</a>. Natural gas is also well suited for a combined use in association with <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> sources such as wind or <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and for alimenting <a href="/wiki/Load_profile" title="Load profile">peak-load</a> power stations functioning in tandem with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric" title="Hydroelectric">hydroelectric</a> plants. Most grid <a href="/wiki/Peaking_power_plant" title="Peaking power plant">peaking power plants</a> and some off-grid <a href="/wiki/Engine-generator" title="Engine-generator">engine-generators</a> use natural gas. Particularly high efficiencies can be achieved through combining gas turbines with a steam turbine in <a href="/wiki/Combined_cycle" title="Combined cycle">combined cycle</a> mode. Natural gas burns more cleanly than other fuels, such as oil and coal. Because burning natural gas produces both water and carbon dioxide, it produces less carbon dioxide per unit of energy released than coal, which produces mostly carbon dioxide. Burning natural gas produces only about half the carbon dioxide per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hour</a> (kWh) that <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> does.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGandE_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGandE-62">[62]</a></sup> For transportation, burning natural gas produces about 30% less carbon dioxide than burning <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>. The US <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a> reports the following emissions in million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">metric tons</a> of carbon dioxide in the world for 2012:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="date in article:2012, archive date:2011, report for year 2006 (November 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Natural gas: 6,799</li>
<li>Petroleum: 11,695</li>
<li>Coal: 13,787</li></ul>
<p>Coal-fired electric power generation emits around 2,000 pounds (900 kg) of carbon dioxide for every <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt-hour" title="Megawatt-hour">megawatt-hour</a> (MWh) generated, which is almost double the carbon dioxide released by natural gas-fired generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Because of this higher carbon efficiency of natural gas generation, as the fuel mix in the United States has changed to reduce coal and increase natural gas generation, carbon dioxide emissions have unexpectedly fallen. Those measured in the first quarter of 2012 were the lowest of any recorded for the first quarter of any year since 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Combined cycle power generation using natural gas is currently the cleanest available source of power using hydrocarbon fuels, and this technology is widely and increasingly used as natural gas can be obtained at increasingly reasonable costs. <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">Fuel cell</a> technology may eventually provide cleaner options for converting natural gas into electricity, but as yet it is not <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_prices" title="Natural gas prices">price-competitive</a>.  Locally produced electricity and heat using natural gas powered Combined Heat and Power plant (CHP or <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">Cogeneration</a> plant) is considered energy efficient and a rapid way to cut carbon emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alfagy_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alfagy-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural gas generated power has increased from 740 TWh in 1973 to 5140 TWh in 2014, generating 22% of the worlds total electricity. Approximately half as much as generated with coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (November 2017)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (November 2017)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Efforts around the world to reduce the use of coal has led some regions to <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_phase-out#Switch_to_natural_gas" title="Fossil fuel phase-out">switch to natural gas</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_use">Domestic use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Domestic use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas dispensed in a residential setting can generate temperatures in excess of 1,100 °C (2,000 °F) making it a powerful domestic cooking and heating fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In much of the developed world it is supplied through pipes to homes, where it is used for many purposes including ranges and ovens, gas-heated <a href="/wiki/Clothes_dryer" title="Clothes dryer">clothes dryers</a>, <a href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">heating</a>/<a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">cooling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Central_heating" title="Central heating">central heating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AtoZ_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-AtoZ-69">[69]</a></sup> Heaters in homes and other buildings may include boilers, <a href="/wiki/Furnace" title="Furnace">furnaces</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_heater" title="Water heater">water heaters</a>. Both North America and Europe are major consumers of natural gas.
</p><p>Domestic appliances, furnaces, and boilers use low pressure, usually 6 to 7 <a href="/wiki/Inch_of_water" title="Inch of water">inches of water</a> (6" to 7" WC), which is about 0.25 psig. The pressures in the supply lines vary, either utilization pressure (UP, the aforementioned 6" to 7" WC) or elevated pressure (EP), which may be anywhere from 1 psig to 120 psig. Systems using EP have a <a href="/wiki/Pressure_regulator" title="Pressure regulator">regulator</a> at the service entrance to step down the pressure to UP.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the US <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> (CNG) is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a> homes without connections to <a href="/wiki/Plumbing" title="Plumbing">piped</a>-in <a href="/wiki/Public_utility" title="Public utility">public utility</a> services, or with portable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grill_(cooking)" title="Grill (cooking)">grills</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Natural gas is also supplied by independent natural gas suppliers through <a href="/wiki/Natural_Gas_Choice" title="Natural Gas Choice">Natural Gas Choice</a> programs throughout the United States. However, as CNG costs more than LPG (<a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a>), LPG is the dominant source of rural gas.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WMATA_3006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/WMATA_3006.jpg/220px-WMATA_3006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/WMATA_3006.jpg/330px-WMATA_3006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/WMATA_3006.jpg/440px-WMATA_3006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WMATA_3006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> <a href="/wiki/Metrobus_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Metrobus (Washington, D.C.)">Metrobus</a>, which runs on natural gas.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>CNG is a cleaner and also cheaper alternative to other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobile</a> fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> (petrol) and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a>. By the end of 2014 there were over 20 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicles" title="Natural gas vehicles">natural gas vehicles</a> worldwide, led by <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (3.5 million), <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (3.3 million), <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> (2.8 million), <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> (2.5 million), <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (1.8 million), and <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> (1.8 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The energy efficiency is generally equal to that of gasoline engines, but lower compared with modern diesel engines. Gasoline/petrol vehicles converted to run on natural gas suffer because of the low <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratio</a> of their engines, resulting in a cropping of delivered power while running on natural gas (10%–15%). CNG-specific engines, however, use a higher compression ratio due to this fuel's higher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octane_number" title="Octane number">octane number</a> of 120–130.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Besides use in road vehicles, CNG can also be used in aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Compressed natural gas has been used in some aircraft like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aviat_Aircraft" title="Aviat Aircraft">Aviat Aircraft</a> Husky 200 CNG<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> and the Chromarat VX-1 KittyHawk<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>LNG is also being used in aircraft. <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> aircraft manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Tupolev" title="Tupolev">Tupolev</a> for instance is running a development program to produce LNG- and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>-powered aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The program has been running since the mid-1970s, and seeks to develop LNG and hydrogen variants of the <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-204" title="Tupolev Tu-204">Tu-204</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-334" title="Tupolev Tu-334">Tu-334</a> passenger aircraft, and also the <a href="/wiki/Tupolev_Tu-330" title="Tupolev Tu-330">Tu-330</a> cargo aircraft. Depending on the current market price for jet fuel and LNG, fuel for an LNG-powered aircraft could cost 5,000 <a href="/wiki/Russian_ruble" title="Russian ruble">rubles</a> (US$100) less per tonne, roughly 60%, with considerable reductions to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxide</a> emissions.
</p><p>The advantages of liquid methane as a jet engine fuel are that it has more specific energy than the standard <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> mixes do and that its low temperature can help cool the air which the engine compresses for greater volumetric efficiency, in effect replacing an <a href="/wiki/Intercooler" title="Intercooler">intercooler</a>. Alternatively, it can be used to lower the temperature of the exhaust.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertilizers">Fertilizers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fertilizers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas is a major feedstock for the production of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, via the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber process</a>, for use in <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AtoZ_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-AtoZ-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen">Hydrogen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Hydrogen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Industrial_gas" title="Industrial gas">Industrial gas</a></div>
<p>Natural gas can be used to produce <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, with one common method being the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_reformer" title="Hydrogen reformer">hydrogen reformer</a>. Hydrogen has many applications: it is a primary feedstock for the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemical industry</a>, a hydrogenating agent, an important commodity for oil refineries, and the fuel source in <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_vehicle" title="Hydrogen vehicle">hydrogen vehicles</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_and_fish_feed">Animal and fish feed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Animal and fish feed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Protein rich animal and fish feed is produced by feeding natural gas to <a href="/wiki/Methylococcus_capsulatus" title="Methylococcus capsulatus">Methylococcus capsulatus</a> bacteria on commercial scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas is also used in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">fabrics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paint</a>, and other products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AtoZ_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-AtoZ-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Environmental effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_the_energy_industry" title="Environmental impact of the energy industry">Environmental impact of the energy industry</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_of_natural_gas_release">Effect of natural gas release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Effect of natural gas release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">Atmospheric methane</a></div>
<p>Natural gas is mainly composed of methane. After release to the atmosphere it is removed by gradual oxidation to carbon dioxide and water by hydroxyl radicals (<span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) formed in the troposphere or stratosphere, giving the overall chemical reaction <span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manahan_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manahan-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_GISS_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_GISS-81">[81]</a></sup> While the lifetime of atmospheric methane is relatively short when compared to carbon dioxide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ny_times_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ny_times-82">[82]</a></sup> with a half-life of about 7 years, it is more efficient at trapping heat in the atmosphere, so that a given quantity of methane has 84 times the global-warming potential of carbon dioxide over a 20-year period and 28 times over a 100-year period. Natural gas is thus a more potent <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> than carbon dioxide due to the greater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global-warming_potential" title="Global-warming potential">global-warming potential</a> of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-83">[83]</a></sup> 2009 estimates by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">EPA</a> place global emissions of methane at 85 cubic kilometers (3.0 trillion cubic feet) annually,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ny_times_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ny_times-82">[82]</a></sup> or 3% of global production, 3.0 trillion cubic meters or 105 trillion cubic feet (2009 est).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Direct emissions of methane represented 14.3% by volume of all global anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>During extraction, storage, transportation, and distribution, natural gas is known to leak into the atmosphere, particularly during the extraction process. A <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> study in 2011 demonstrated that the leak rate of methane may be high enough to jeopardize its global warming advantage over coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> This study was criticized later for its over-estimation of methane leakage values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Response_to_Cornell_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Response_to_Cornell-87">[87]</a></sup> Preliminary results of some air sampling from airplanes done by the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration indicated higher-than-estimated methane releases by gas wells in some areas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Business_week_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Business_week-88">[88]</a></sup> but the overall results showed <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a> in line with previous EPA estimates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide_emissions">Carbon dioxide emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Carbon dioxide emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas is often described as the cleanest fossil <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a>. It produces 25%–30% and 40%–45% less carbon dioxide per <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joule</a> delivered than oil and coal respectively,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGandE_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-NGandE-62">[62]</a></sup> and potentially fewer <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a> than other hydrocarbon fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGandE_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-NGandE-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGinASIA_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGinASIA-90">[90]</a></sup> However, in absolute terms, it comprises a substantial percentage of human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emission" title="Carbon emission">carbon emissions</a>, and this contribution is projected to grow. According to the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">IPCC Fourth Assessment Report</a>, in 2004, natural gas produced about 5.3 billion tons a year of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> emissions, while coal and oil produced 10.6 and 10.2 billion tons respectively. According to an updated version of the <a href="/wiki/Special_Report_on_Emissions_Scenarios#B2" title="Special Report on Emissions Scenarios">Special Report on Emissions Scenario</a> by 2030, natural gas would be the source of 11 billion tons a year, with coal and oil now 8.4 and 17.2 billion respectively because demand is increasing 1.9% a year.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_pollutants">Other pollutants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Other pollutants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas produces far lower amounts of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxides</a> than other fossil fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGinASIA_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-NGinASIA-90">[90]</a></sup> The pollutants due to natural gas combustion are listed below:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGandE_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-NGandE-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GvsC_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-GvsC-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
