</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radionuclides">Radionuclides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Radionuclides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas extraction also produces radioactive isotopes of <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a> (Po-210), <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> (Pb-210) and <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a> (Rn-220). Radon is a gas with initial activity from 5 to 200,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Becquerels" title="Becquerels">becquerels</a> per cubic meter of gas. It decays rapidly to Pb-210 which can build up as a thin film in gas extraction equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_concerns">Safety concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Safety concerns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG/220px-Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG/330px-Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG/440px-Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gas_pipeline_odourant_injection_facility.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pipeline odorant injection station</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some gas fields yield <a href="/wiki/Sour_gas" title="Sour gas">sour gas</a> containing <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span>). This untreated gas is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">toxic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Amine_gas_treating" title="Amine gas treating">Amine gas treating</a>, an industrial scale process which removes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acidic" title="Acidic">acidic</a> <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gaseous</a> components, is often used to remove hydrogen sulfide from natural gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Processing_Natural_Gas_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Processing_Natural_Gas-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Extraction of natural gas (or oil) leads to decrease in pressure in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_reservoir" title="Oil reservoir">reservoir</a>. Such decrease in pressure in turn may result in <a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">subsidence</a>, sinking of the ground above. Subsidence may affect ecosystems, waterways, sewer and water supply systems, foundations, and so on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fracking">Fracking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Fracking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_hydraulic_fracturing" title="Environmental impact of hydraulic fracturing">Environmental impact of hydraulic fracturing</a></div>
<p>Releasing natural gas from subsurface porous rock formations may be accomplished by a process called <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> or "fracking". It's estimated that hydraulic fracturing will eventually account for nearly 70% of natural gas development in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Since the first commercial hydraulic fracturing operation in 1949, approximately one million wells have been hydraulically fractured in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The production of natural gas from hydraulically fractured wells has utilized the technological developments of directional and horizontal drilling, which improved access to natural gas in tight rock formations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Strong growth in the production of unconventional gas from hydraulically fractured wells occurred between 2000-2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>In hydraulic fracturing, well operators force water mixed with a variety of chemicals through the wellbore casing into the rock. The high pressure water breaks up or "fracks" the rock, which releases gas from the rock formation. Sand and other particles are added to the water as a proppant to keep the fractures in the rock open, thus enabling the gas to flow into the casing and then to the surface. Chemicals are added to the fluid to perform such functions as reducing friction and inhibiting corrosion. After the "frack," oil or gas is extracted and 30–70% of the frack fluid, i.e. the mixture of water, chemicals, sand, etc., flows back to the surface. Many gas-bearing formations also contain water, which will flow up the wellbore to the surface along with the gas, in both hydraulically fractured and non-hydraulically fractured wells. This <a href="/wiki/Produced_water" title="Produced water">produced water</a> often has a high content of salt and other dissolved minerals that occur in the formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The volume of water used to hydraulically fracture wells varies according to the hydraulic fracturing technique.  In the United States, the average volume of water used per hydraulic fracture has been reported as nearly 7,375 gallons for vertical oil and gas wells prior to 1953, nearly 197,000 gallons for vertical oil and gas wells between 2000-2010, and nearly 3 million gallons for horizontal gas wells between 2000-2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Determining which fracking technique is appropriate for well productivity depends largely on the properties of the reservoir rock from which to extract oil or gas.  If the rock is characterized by low-permeability — which refers to its ability to let substances, i.e. gas, pass through it, then the rock may be considered a source of <a href="/wiki/Tight_gas" title="Tight gas">tight gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Fracking for shale gas, which is currently also known as a source of <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_gas" title="Unconventional gas">unconventional gas</a>, involves drilling a borehole vertically until it reaches a lateral shale rock formation, at which point the drill turns to follow the rock for hundreds or thousands of feet horizontally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In contrast, conventional oil and gas sources are characterized by higher rock permeability, which naturally enables the flow of oil or gas into the wellbore with less intensive hydraulic fracturing techniques than the production of tight gas has required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The decades in development of drilling technology for conventional and <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">unconventional oil</a> and gas production has not only improved access to natural gas in low-permeability reservoir rocks, but also posed significant adverse impacts on environmental and public health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The US EPA has acknowledged that toxic, carcinogenic chemicals, i.e. benzene and ethylbenzene, have been used as gelling agents in water and chemical mixtures for high volume horizontal fracturing (HVHF).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Following the hydraulic fracture in HVHF, the water, chemicals, and frack fluid that return to the well's surface, called flowback or produced water, may contain radioactive materials, heavy metals, natural salts, and hydrocarbons which exist naturally in shale rock formations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Fracking chemicals, radioactive materials, heavy metals, and salts that are removed from the HVHF well by well operators are so difficult to remove from the water they're mixed with, and would so heavily <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">pollute</a> the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>, that most of the flowback is either recycled into other fracking operations or injected into deep underground wells, eliminating the water that HVHF required from the hydrologic cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Added_odor">Added odor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Added odor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In order to assist in detecting <a href="/wiki/Leak" title="Leak">leaks</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Odorizer" title="Odorizer">odorizer</a> is added to the otherwise colorless and almost odorless gas used by consumers. The odor has been compared to the smell of rotten eggs, due to the added <a href="/wiki/Tert-Butylthiol" title="Tert-Butylthiol">tert-Butylthiol</a> (t-butyl mercaptan). Sometimes a related compound, <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrothiophene" title="Tetrahydrothiophene">thiophane</a>, may be used in the mixture. Situations in which an odorant that is added to natural gas can be detected by analytical instrumentation, but cannot be properly detected by an observer with a normal sense of smell, have occurred in the natural gas industry.  This is caused by odor masking, when one odorant overpowers the sensation of another. As of 2011, the industry is conducting research on the causes of odor masking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_of_explosion">Risk of explosion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Risk of explosion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fire_engines_in_Kiev,_Ukraine.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG/220px-Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG/330px-Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG/440px-Fire_engines_in_Kiev%2C_Ukraine.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fire_engines_in_Kiev,_Ukraine.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gas network emergency vehicle responding to a major fire in <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a></div></div></div>
<p>Explosions caused by natural <a href="/wiki/Gas_leak" title="Gas leak">gas leaks</a> occur a few times each year.  Individual homes, small businesses and other structures are most frequently affected when an internal leak builds up gas inside the structure.  Frequently, the blast is powerful enough to significantly damage a building but leave it standing.  In these cases, the people inside tend to have minor to moderate injuries.  Occasionally, the gas can collect in high enough quantities to cause a deadly explosion, disintegrating one or more buildings in the process.  The gas usually dissipates readily outdoors, but can sometimes collect in dangerous quantities if flow rates are high enough.  However, considering the tens of millions of structures that use the fuel, the individual risk of using natural gas is very low.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_of_carbon_monoxide_inhalation">Risk of carbon monoxide inhalation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Risk of carbon monoxide inhalation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural gas heating systems may cause <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">carbon monoxide poisoning</a> if unvented or poorly vented. In 2011, natural gas furnaces, space heaters, water heaters and stoves were blamed for 11 carbon monoxide deaths in the US. Another 22 deaths were attributed to appliances running on liquified petroleum gas, and 17 deaths on gas of unspecified type. Improvements in natural gas furnace designs have greatly reduced CO poisoning concerns. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_detector" title="Carbon monoxide detector">Detectors</a> are also available that warn of carbon monoxide and/or explosive gas (methane, propane, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Energy_content.2C_statistics.2C_and_pricing"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_content,_statistics,_and_pricing">Energy content, statistics, and pricing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Energy content, statistics, and pricing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_prices" title="Natural gas prices">Natural gas prices</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Billion_cubic_metres_of_natural_gas" title="Billion cubic metres of natural gas">Billion cubic metres of natural gas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="517" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg/330px-Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg/495px-Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg/660px-Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_hub_NG_prices.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_prices" title="Natural gas prices">Natural gas prices</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hub" title="Henry Hub">Henry Hub</a> in US dollars per million BTUs ($/mmbtu).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="566" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png/330px-Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png/495px-Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Natural_Gas_Price_Comparison.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of natural gas prices in Japan, United Kingdom, and United States, 2007–2011</div></div></div>
<p>Quantities of natural gas are measured in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal_cubic_meter" title="Normal cubic meter">normal cubic meters</a> (cubic meter of gas at "normal" temperature 0 °C (32 °F) and pressure 101.325 kPa (14.6959 psi)) or <a href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_foot" title="Standard cubic foot">standard cubic feet</a> (cubic foot of gas at "standard" temperature 60.0 °F (15.6 °C) and pressure 14.73 psi (101.6 kPa)), one cubic meter ≈ 35.3147 cu ft. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Higher_heating_value" title="Higher heating value">gross heat of combustion</a> of commercial quality natural gas is around 39 MJ/m<sup>3</sup> (0.31 kWh/cu ft), but this can vary by several percent. This is about 49 MJ/kg (6.2 kWh/lb) (assuming a density of 0.8 kg/m<sup>3</sup> (0.05 lb/cu ft),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> an approximate value).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: European Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gas prices for end users vary greatly across the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> A single European energy market, one of the key objectives of the EU, should level the prices of gas in all EU member states. Moreover, it would help to resolve supply and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> issues,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> as well as strengthen relations with other Mediterranean countries and foster investments in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="750" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg/330px-US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg/495px-US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg/660px-US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Natural_Gas_Production.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>US Natural Gas Marketed Production 1900 to 2012 (US EIA data)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="2025" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png/330px-Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png/495px-Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png/660px-Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gas_Production_Top_5_Countries.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trends in the top five natural gas-producing countries (US EIA data)</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">US units</a>, one standard cubic foot (28 L) of natural gas produces around 1,028 <a href="/wiki/British_thermal_unit" title="British thermal unit">British thermal units</a> (1,085 kJ). The actual heating value when the water formed does not condense is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_heating_value" title="Lower heating value">net heat of combustion</a> and can be as much as 10% less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, retail sales are often in units of <a href="/wiki/Therm" title="Therm">therms</a> (th); 1 therm = 100,000 BTU. Gas sales to domestic consumers are often in units of 100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_cubic_feet" title="Standard cubic feet">standard cubic feet</a> (scf). <a href="/wiki/Gas_meter" title="Gas meter">Gas meters</a> measure the volume of gas used, and this is converted to therms by multiplying the volume by the energy content of the gas used during that period, which varies slightly over time. The typical annual consumption of a single family residence is 1,000 therms or one <a href="/wiki/Residential_Customer_Equivalent" title="Residential Customer Equivalent">Residential Customer Equivalent</a> (RCE). Wholesale transactions are generally done in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decatherm" title="Decatherm">decatherms</a> (Dth), thousand decatherms (MDth), or million decatherms (MMDth). A million decatherms is a trillion BTU, roughly a billion cubic feet of natural gas.
</p><p>The price of natural gas varies greatly depending on location and type of consumer. In 2007, a price of $7 per 1000 cubic feet ($0.25/m<sup>3</sup>) was typical in the United States. The typical caloric value of natural gas is roughly 1,000 BTU per cubic foot, depending on gas composition. This corresponds to around $7 per million BTU or around $7 per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigajoule" title="Gigajoule">gigajoule</a> (GJ). In April 2008, the wholesale price was $10 per 1000 cubic feet ($10/MMBTU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The residential price varies from 50% to 300% more than the wholesale price. At the end of 2007, this was $12–$16 per 1000 cubic feet ($0.42–$0.57/m<sup>3</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Natural gas in the United States is traded as a <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures contract</a> on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mercantile_Exchange" title="New York Mercantile Exchange">New York Mercantile Exchange</a>. Each contract is for 10,000 MMBTU or 10 billion BTU (10,551 GJ). Thus, if the price of gas is $10/MMBTU on the NYMEX, the contract is worth $100,000.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Canada uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_units" title="Metric units">metric</a> measure for internal trade of petrochemical products. Consequently, natural gas is sold by the gigajoule (GJ), cubic meter (m<sup>3</sup>) or thousand cubic meters (E3m3). Distribution infrastructure and meters almost always meter volume (cubic foot or cubic meter). Some jurisdictions, such as Saskatchewan, sell gas by volume only. Other jurisdictions, such as Alberta, gas is sold by the energy content (GJ). In these areas, almost all meters for residential and small commercial customers measure volume (m<sup>3</sup> or ft<sup>3</sup>), and billing statements include a multiplier to convert the volume to energy content of the local gas supply.
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigajoule" title="Gigajoule">gigajoule</a> (GJ) is a measure approximately equal to half a barrel (250 lbs) of oil, or 1 million BTUs, or 1,000 cu ft or 28 m<sup>3</sup> of gas. The energy content of gas supply in Canada can vary from 37 to 43 MJ/m<sup>3</sup> (990 to 1,150 BTU/cu ft) depending on gas supply and processing between the wellhead and the customer.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elsewhere">Elsewhere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Elsewhere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the rest of the world, natural gas is sold in gigajoule retail units. LNG (liquefied natural gas) and LPG (<a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a>) are traded in metric tonnes (1,000 kg) or MMBTU as spot deliveries. Long term natural gas distribution contracts are signed in cubic meters, and LNG contracts are in metric tonnes. The LNG and LPG is transported by specialized <a href="/wiki/LNG_carrier" title="LNG carrier">transport ships</a>, as the gas is liquified at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> temperatures. The specification of each LNG/LPG cargo will usually contain the energy content, but this information is in general not available to the public.
</p><p>In the Russian Federation, <a href="/wiki/Gazprom" title="Gazprom">Gazprom</a> sold approximately 250 billion cubic meters (8.8 trillion cubic feet) of natural gas in 2008. In 2013 they produced 487.4 billion cubic meters (17.21 trillion cubic feet) of natural and associated gas. Gazprom supplied Europe with 161.5 billion cubic meters (5.70 trillion cubic feet) of gas in 2013.
</p><p>In August 2015, possibly the largest natural gas discovery in history was made and notified by an Italian gas company ENI. The energy company indicated that it has unearthed a <a href="/wiki/Giant_oil_and_gas_fields" title="Giant oil and gas fields">"supergiant" gas field</a> in the Mediterranean Sea covering about 40 square miles (100 km<sup>2</sup>). It was also reported that the gas field could hold a potential 30 trillion cubic feet (850 billion cubic meters) of natural gas. ENI said that the energy is about5.5 billion barrels of oil equivalent [BOE] (3.4<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>10</sup> GJ). The field was found in the deep waters off the northern coast of Egypt and ENI claims that it will be the largest ever in the Mediterranean and even the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_gas_as_an_asset_class_for_institutional_investors">Natural gas as an asset class for institutional investors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Natural gas as an asset class for institutional investors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Research conducted by the <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forum_Mondial_des_Fonds_de_Pension" title="fr:Forum Mondial des Fonds de Pension">World Pensions Council (WPC)</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (March 2014)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> suggests that large US and Canadian pension funds and Asian and <a href="/wiki/MENA" title="MENA">MENA</a> area <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_wealth_fund" title="Sovereign wealth fund">SWF</a> investors have become particularly active in the fields of natural gas and natural gas infrastructure, a trend started in 2005 by the formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scotia_Gas_Networks" title="Scotia Gas Networks">Scotia Gas Networks</a> in the UK by <a href="/wiki/OMERS" title="OMERS">OMERS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Teachers%27_Pension_Plan" title="Ontario Teachers' Pension Plan">Ontario Teachers' Pension Plan</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Adsorbed_natural_gas_.28ANG.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Adsorbed_natural_gas_(ANG)">Adsorbed natural gas (ANG)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Adsorbed natural gas (ANG)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Another way to store natural gas is adsorbing it to the porous solids called sorbents. The best condition for methane storage is at room temperature and atmospheric pressure. The used pressure can be up to 4 MPa (about 40 times atmospheric pressure) for having more storage capacity. The most common sorbent used for ANG is activated carbon (AC). Three main types of activated carbons for ANG are: Activated Carbon Fiber (ACF), Powdered Activated Carbon (PAC), activated carbon monolith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
